Economy {#Economy}
------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&action=edit&section=27 "Edit section: Economy")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Mexico_City_Reforma_Street.jpg/220px-Mexico_City_Reforma_Street.jpg)](/wiki/File:Mexico_City_Reforma_Street.jpg) The [Paseo de la Reforma](/wiki/Paseo_de_la_Reforma "Paseo de la Reforma") is a wide avenue designed by [Ferdinand von Rosenzweig](/wiki/Ferdinand_von_Rosenzweig "Ferdinand von Rosenzweig") in the 1860s and was modeled after the [Champs-Élysées](/wiki/Champs-%C3%89lys%C3%A9es "Champs-Élysées") in Paris.^[\[163\]](#cite_note-167)^

Mexico City is one of the most important economic hubs in [Latin America](/wiki/Latin_America "Latin America"). The city proper produces 15.8% of the country's [gross domestic product](/wiki/Gross_domestic_product "Gross domestic product").^[\[164\]](#cite_note-INEGI-168)^ In 2002, Mexico City had a [Human Development Index](/wiki/Human_Development_Index "Human Development Index") score of 0.915,^[\[165\]](#cite_note-169)^ identical to that of [South Korea](/wiki/International_rankings_of_South_Korea "International rankings of South Korea"). In 2007, residents in the top twelve percent of GDP per capita holders in the city had a mean [disposable income](/wiki/Disposable_and_discretionary_income "Disposable and discretionary income") of [US$](/wiki/United_States_dollar "United States dollar")98,517. The high spending power of Mexico City inhabitants makes the city attractive for companies offering prestige and [luxury goods](/wiki/Luxury_goods "Luxury goods"). According to a 2009 study conducted by [PwC](/wiki/PricewaterhouseCoopers "PricewaterhouseCoopers"), Mexico City had a GDP of $390 billion, ranking it as the eighth richest city in the world and the richest in Latin America.^[\[166\]](#cite_note-ukmediacentre.pwc.com-170)^ In 2009, Mexico City alone would rank as the 30th largest economy in the world.^[\[167\]](#cite_note-emporis-171)^{#cite_ref-emporis_171-0}

Mexico City is the greatest contributor to the country's industrial GDP (15.8%) and also the greatest contributor to the country's GDP in the [service sector](/wiki/Tertiary_sector_of_the_economy "Tertiary sector of the economy") (25.3%). Due to the limited non-urbanized space at the south---most of which is protected through environmental laws---the contribution of Mexico City in agriculture is the smallest of all federal entities in the country.^[\[164\]](#cite_note-INEGI-168)^ The economic reforms of President [Carlos Salinas de Gortari](/wiki/Carlos_Salinas_de_Gortari "Carlos Salinas de Gortari") had a tremendous effect on the city, as a number of businesses, including banks and airlines, were privatized. He also signed the [North American Free Trade Agreement](/wiki/North_American_Free_Trade_Agreement "North American Free Trade Agreement") (NAFTA). This led to decentralization^[\[168\]](#cite_note-furness-172)^ and a shift in Mexico City's economic base, from manufacturing to services, as most factories moved away to either the [State of Mexico](/wiki/State_of_Mexico "State of Mexico"), or more commonly to the northern border. By contrast, corporate office buildings set their base in the city.{#cite_ref-furness_172-0}
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/MexicoCityStockExchange01.jpg/170px-MexicoCityStockExchange01.jpg)](/wiki/File:MexicoCityStockExchange01.jpg) [Mexican Stock Exchange](/wiki/Mexican_Stock_Exchange "Mexican Stock Exchange")

Mexico City offers an immense and varied consumer retail market, ranging from basic foods to ultra high-end luxury goods. Consumers may buy in [fixed indoor markets](/wiki/Traditional_fixed_markets_in_Mexico "Traditional fixed markets in Mexico"), in [mobile markets (*tianguis*)](/wiki/Tianguis "Tianguis"), from [street vendors](/wiki/Street_vendors_in_Mexico_City "Street vendors in Mexico City"), from downtown shops in a street dedicated to a certain type of good, in convenience stores and traditional neighborhood stores, in modern supermarkets, in warehouse and membership stores and the shopping centers that they anchor, in department stores, in [big-box stores](/wiki/Big-box_store "Big-box store"), and in modern shopping malls. In addition, "[tianguis](/wiki/Tianguis "Tianguis")" or mobile markets set up shop on streets in many neighborhoods, depending on day of week. Sundays see the largest number of these markets.

The city's main source of fresh produce is the [Central de Abasto](/wiki/Central_de_Abasto "Central de Abasto"). This in itself is a self-contained mini-city in [Iztapalapa](/wiki/Iztapalapa "Iztapalapa") borough covering an area equivalent to several dozen city blocks. The wholesale market supplies most of the city's "mercados", supermarkets and restaurants, as well as people who come to buy the produce for themselves. Tons of fresh produce are trucked in from all over Mexico every day. The principal fish market is known as [La Nueva Viga](/wiki/La_Nueva_Viga_Market "La Nueva Viga Market"), in the same complex as the Central de Abastos.^[\[169\]](#cite_note-actecon-173)^ The world-renowned market of [Tepito](/wiki/Tepito "Tepito") occupies 25 blocks, and sells a variety of products. A staple for consumers in the city is the omnipresent "mercado". Every major neighborhood in the city has its own borough-regulated market, often more than one. These are large well-established facilities offering most basic products, such as fresh produce and meat/poultry, dry goods, tortillerías, and many other services such as locksmiths, herbal medicine, hardware goods, sewing implements; and a multitude of stands offering freshly made, home-style cooking and drinks in the tradition of [aguas frescas](/wiki/Aguas_frescas "Aguas frescas") and [atole](/wiki/Atole "Atole").{#cite_ref-actecon_173-0}

Street vendors ply their trade from stalls in the *tianguis* as well as at non-officially controlled concentrations around metro stations and hospitals; at *plazas comerciales* , where vendors of a certain "theme" (e.g. stationery) are housed; originally these were organized to accommodate vendors formerly selling on the street; or simply from improvised stalls on a city sidewalk.^[\[170\]](#cite_note-pena-174)^ In addition, food and goods are sold from people walking with baskets, pushing carts, from bicycles or the backs of trucks, or simply from a tarp or cloth laid on the ground.^[\[171\]](#cite_note-175)^ In the center of the city informal street vendors are increasingly targeted by laws and prosecution.^[\[155\]](#cite_note-mueller-159)^ The weekly [San Felipe de Jesús Tianguis](/wiki/San_Felipe_de_Jes%C3%BAs_Tianguis "San Felipe de Jesús Tianguis") is reported to be the largest in Latin America.^[\[172\]](#cite_note-176)^{#cite_ref-176}

The [Historic Center of Mexico City](/wiki/Historic_Center_of_Mexico_City "Historic Center of Mexico City") is widely known for specialized, often low-cost retailers. Certain blocks or streets are dedicated to shops selling a certain type of merchandise, with areas dedicated to over 40 categories such as home appliances, lamps and electricals, closets and bathrooms, housewares, wedding dresses, jukeboxes, printing, office furniture and safes, books, photography, jewelry, and opticians.^[\[173\]](#cite_note-177)^{#cite_ref-177}  
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Vista_panor%C3%A1mica_del_lago_en_el_Parque_La_Mexicana.jpg/700px-Vista_panor%C3%A1mica_del_lago_en_el_Parque_La_Mexicana.jpg)](/wiki/File:Vista_panor%C3%A1mica_del_lago_en_el_Parque_La_Mexicana.jpg "Santa Fe is one of the centers of greatest economic activity in the city.")  
[](/wiki/File:Vista_panor%C3%A1mica_del_lago_en_el_Parque_La_Mexicana.jpg "File:Vista panorámica del lago en el Parque La Mexicana.jpg")
[Santa Fe](/wiki/Santa_Fe,_Mexico_City "Santa Fe, Mexico City") is one of the centers of greatest economic activity in the city.^[\[174\]](#cite_note-178)^  

### Tourism {#Tourism}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&action=edit&section=28 "Edit section: Tourism")\]  
See also: [Barrios Mágicos of Mexico City](/wiki/Barrios_M%C3%A1gicos_of_Mexico_City "Barrios Mágicos of Mexico City")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Turibus_Ciudad_de_Mexico.jpg/170px-Turibus_Ciudad_de_Mexico.jpg)](/wiki/File:Turibus_Ciudad_de_Mexico.jpg) The Turibus runs through many of the most important tourist attractions in the city.

Mexico City is a destination for many foreign tourists. The [Historic center of Mexico City](/wiki/Historic_center_of_Mexico_City "Historic center of Mexico City") (*Centro Histórico* ) and the "floating gardens" of [Xochimilco](/wiki/Xochimilco "Xochimilco") in the southern borough have been declared [World Heritage Sites](/wiki/World_Heritage_Site "World Heritage Site") by [UNESCO](/wiki/UNESCO "UNESCO"). Landmarks in the Historic Center include the [Plaza de la Constitución](/wiki/Z%C3%B3calo "Zócalo") (Zócalo), the main central square with its epoch-contrasting Spanish-era [Metropolitan Cathedral](/wiki/Mexico_City_Metropolitan_Cathedral "Mexico City Metropolitan Cathedral") and [National Palace](/wiki/National_Palace_(Mexico) "National Palace (Mexico)"), ancient Aztec temple ruins [Templo Mayor](/wiki/Templo_Mayor "Templo Mayor") ("Major Temple") and modern structures, all within a few steps of one another. (The Templo Mayor was discovered in 1978 while workers were digging to place underground electric cables).

The most recognizable icon of Mexico City is the golden [Angel of Independence](/wiki/Angel_of_Independence "Angel of Independence") on the wide, elegant avenue [Paseo de la Reforma](/wiki/Paseo_de_la_Reforma "Paseo de la Reforma"), modeled by the order of the Emperor [Maximilian of Mexico](/wiki/Maximilian_I_of_Mexico "Maximilian I of Mexico") after the [Champs-Élysées](/wiki/Champs-%C3%89lys%C3%A9es "Champs-Élysées") in Paris. This avenue was designed over the Americas' oldest known major roadway in the 19th century to connect the [National Palace](/wiki/National_Palace_(Mexico) "National Palace (Mexico)") (seat of government) with the [Castle of Chapultepec](/wiki/Chapultepec_Castle "Chapultepec Castle"), the imperial residence. Today, this avenue is an important financial district in which the [Mexican Stock Exchange](/wiki/Mexican_Stock_Exchange "Mexican Stock Exchange") and several [corporate headquarters](/wiki/Corporate_headquarters "Corporate headquarters") are located. Another important avenue is the [Avenida de los Insurgentes](/wiki/Avenida_de_los_Insurgentes "Avenida de los Insurgentes"), which extends 28.8 km (17.9 mi) and is one of the longest single avenues in the world.

[Chapultepec](/wiki/Chapultepec "Chapultepec") Park houses the [Chapultepec Castle](/wiki/Chapultepec_Castle "Chapultepec Castle"), now a museum on a hill that overlooks the park and its numerous museums, monuments and the national zoo and the [National Museum of Anthropology](/wiki/Museo_Nacional_de_Antropolog%C3%ADa "Museo Nacional de Antropología") (which houses the [Aztec Calendar Stone](/wiki/Aztec_calendar_stone "Aztec calendar stone")).
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Aztec_Stone_of_the_Sun_%282088976462%29.jpg/170px-Aztec_Stone_of_the_Sun_%282088976462%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Aztec_Stone_of_the_Sun_(2088976462).jpg) The [Aztec sun stone](/wiki/Aztec_sun_stone "Aztec sun stone") in the [National Museum of Anthropology](/wiki/National_Museum_of_Anthropology_(Mexico) "National Museum of Anthropology (Mexico)")

Another piece of architecture is the [Palacio de Bellas Artes](/wiki/Palacio_de_Bellas_Artes "Palacio de Bellas Artes"), a white marble theater/museum whose weight is such that it has gradually been sinking into the soft ground below. Its construction began during the presidency of [Porfirio Díaz](/wiki/Porfirio_D%C3%ADaz "Porfirio Díaz") and ended in 1934, after being interrupted by the [Mexican Revolution](/wiki/Mexican_Revolution "Mexican Revolution") in the 1920s.

The [Plaza de las Tres Culturas](/wiki/Plaza_de_las_Tres_Culturas "Plaza de las Tres Culturas"), in this square are located the [College of Santa Cruz de Tlatelolco](/wiki/Colegio_de_Santa_Cruz_de_Tlatelolco "Colegio de Santa Cruz de Tlatelolco"), that is [the first and oldest](/wiki/List_of_oldest_buildings_in_the_Americas "List of oldest buildings in the Americas") European school of higher learning in the [Americas](/wiki/Americas "Americas"),^[\[133\]](#cite_note-catalog.hathitrust.org-137)^ and the archeological site of the [city-state of Tlatelolco](/wiki/Tlatelolco_(archaeological_site) "Tlatelolco (archaeological site)"), and the shrine and [Basilica of Our Lady of Guadalupe](/wiki/Basilica_of_Our_Lady_of_Guadalupe "Basilica of Our Lady of Guadalupe") are also important sites. There is a [double-decker bus](/wiki/Double-decker_bus "Double-decker bus"), known as the "Turibus", that circles most of these sites, and has timed audio describing the sites in multiple languages as they are passed.{#cite_ref-catalog.hathitrust.org_137-1}

In addition, according to the Secretariat of Tourism, the city has about 170 [museums](/wiki/Museums "Museums")---is among the top ten of cities in the world with highest number of museums^[\[175\]](#cite_note-179)^---over 100 [art galleries](/wiki/Art_museum "Art museum"), and some 30 [concert halls](/wiki/List_of_concert_halls "List of concert halls"), all of which maintain a constant cultural activity during the whole year. Many areas (e.g. Palacio Nacional and the [National Institute of Cardiology](/w/index.php?title=National_Institute_of_Cardiology&action=edit&redlink=1 "National Institute of Cardiology (page does not exist)")) have murals painted by [Diego Rivera](/wiki/Diego_Rivera "Diego Rivera"). He and his wife [Frida Kahlo](/wiki/Frida_Kahlo "Frida Kahlo") lived in [Coyoacán](/wiki/Coyoac%C3%A1n "Coyoacán"), where several of their homes, studios, and art collections are open to the public. The house where [Leon Trotsky](/wiki/Leon_Trotsky "Leon Trotsky") was initially granted asylum and finally murdered in 1940 is also in Coyoacán. In addition, there are several *[haciendas](/wiki/Hacienda "Hacienda")* that are now restaurants, such as the San Ángel Inn, the Hacienda de Tlalpan, Hacienda de Cortés and the Hacienda de los Morales.{#cite_ref-179}  

Transportation {#Transportation}
--------------------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&action=edit&section=29 "Edit section: Transportation")\]  
Main article: [Transportation in Mexico City](/wiki/Transportation_in_Mexico_City "Transportation in Mexico City")  

### Airports {#Airports}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&action=edit&section=30 "Edit section: Airports")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/AICM_AIR_T2.jpg/220px-AICM_AIR_T2.jpg)](/wiki/File:AICM_AIR_T2.jpg) [Mexico City International Airport](/wiki/Mexico_City_International_Airport "Mexico City International Airport")

[Mexico City International Airport](/wiki/Mexico_City_International_Airport "Mexico City International Airport") is Mexico City's primary airport ([IATA Airport Code](/wiki/International_Air_Transport_Association_airport_code "International Air Transport Association airport code"): MEX), and serves as the hub of [Aeroméxico](/wiki/Aerom%C3%A9xico "Aeroméxico") ([Skyteam](/wiki/SkyTeam "SkyTeam")). [Felipe Ángeles International Airport](/wiki/Felipe_%C3%81ngeles_International_Airport "Felipe Ángeles International Airport") ([IATA Airport Code](/wiki/International_Air_Transport_Association_airport_code "International Air Transport Association airport code"): NLU) is Mexico City's secondary airport, and was opened in 2022, rebuilt from the former Santa Lucía Air Force Base. It is located in [Zumpango](/wiki/Zumpango "Zumpango"), [State of Mexico](/wiki/State_of_Mexico "State of Mexico"), 48.8 kilometres (30 mi) north-northeast of the [historic center of Mexico City](/wiki/Historic_center_of_Mexico_City "Historic center of Mexico City") by car.^[\[176\]](#cite_note-180)^{#cite_ref-180}  

### Sistema de Movilidad Integrada {#Sistema_de_Movilidad_Integrada}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&action=edit&section=31 "Edit section: Sistema de Movilidad Integrada")\]

In 2019, the graphic designer [Lance Wyman](/wiki/Lance_Wyman "Lance Wyman") was engaged to create an integrated map of the multimodal public transportation system; he presented a new logo for the [Sistema de Movilidad Integrada](/wiki/Movilidad_Integrada "Movilidad Integrada"), describing eight distinct modes of transportation. The head of the government, [Claudia Sheinbaum](/wiki/Claudia_Sheinbaum "Claudia Sheinbaum"), said the branding would be used for a new single payment card to streamline public transportation fare collection.^[\[177\]](#cite_note-181)^{#cite_ref-181}  

### Metro {#Metro}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&action=edit&section=32 "Edit section: Metro")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/FE-10_06.jpg/220px-FE-10_06.jpg)](/wiki/File:FE-10_06.jpg) [Mexico City Metro](/wiki/Mexico_City_Metro "Mexico City Metro")  
Main article: [Mexico City Metro](/wiki/Mexico_City_Metro "Mexico City Metro")

Mexico City is served by the [Mexico City Metro](/wiki/Mexico_City_Metro "Mexico City Metro"), a 225.9 km (140 mi) [metro](/wiki/Rapid_transit "Rapid transit") system, which is the largest in Latin America. The first portions were opened in 1969 and it has expanded to 12 lines with [195 stations](/wiki/List_of_Mexico_City_metro_stations "List of Mexico City metro stations"), transporting 4.4 million people every day.^[\[178\]](#cite_note-182)^{#cite_ref-182}  

### Tren Suburbano {#Tren_Suburbano}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&action=edit&section=33 "Edit section: Tren Suburbano")\]  
Main article: [Tren Suburbano](/wiki/Tren_Suburbano "Tren Suburbano")

A suburban rail system, the [Tren Suburbano](/wiki/Tren_Suburbano "Tren Suburbano") serves the metropolitan area, beyond the reach of the [metro](/wiki/Mexico_City_Metro "Mexico City Metro"), with one line serving to municipalities such as [Tlalnepantla](/wiki/Tlalnepantla_de_Baz "Tlalnepantla de Baz") and [Cuautitlán Izcalli](/wiki/Cuautitl%C3%A1n_Izcalli,_Mexico_State "Cuautitlán Izcalli, Mexico State"), but with future lines planned to serve e.g. [Chalco](/wiki/Chalco_de_D%C3%ADaz_Covarrubias "Chalco de Díaz Covarrubias") and [La Paz](/wiki/Los_Reyes_Acaquilpan "Los Reyes Acaquilpan").

Electric transport other than the metro also exists, in the form of several [Mexico City trolleybus](/wiki/Trolleybuses_in_Mexico_City "Trolleybuses in Mexico City") routes and the [Xochimilco Light Rail](/wiki/Xochimilco_Light_Rail "Xochimilco Light Rail") line, both of which are operated by [Servicio de Transportes Eléctricos](/wiki/Servicio_de_Transportes_El%C3%A9ctricos "Servicio de Transportes Eléctricos"). The central area's last [streetcar](/wiki/Tram "Tram") line (tramway, or *[tranvía](https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tranv%C3%ADa "es:Tranvía")*) closed in 1979.  

### Bus {#Bus}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&action=edit&section=34 "Edit section: Bus")\]  
Main articles: [Mexico City Metrobús](/wiki/Mexico_City_Metrob%C3%BAs "Mexico City Metrobús") and [Red de Transporte de Pasajeros](/wiki/Red_de_Transporte_de_Pasajeros "Red de Transporte de Pasajeros")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/15-07-17-Mexico-D-F-RalfR-WMA_1274.jpg/220px-15-07-17-Mexico-D-F-RalfR-WMA_1274.jpg)](/wiki/File:15-07-17-Mexico-D-F-RalfR-WMA_1274.jpg) [Metrobús](/wiki/Mexico_City_Metrob%C3%BAs "Mexico City Metrobús") rapid transit bus stop station at [Mina](/wiki/Mina_(Mexico_City_Metrob%C3%BAs) "Mina (Mexico City Metrobús)")

Mexico City has an extensive bus network, consisting of public buses, [bus rapid transit](/wiki/Bus_rapid_transit "Bus rapid transit"), and [trolleybuses](/wiki/Trolleybuses "Trolleybuses").  

### Roads {#Roads}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&action=edit&section=35 "Edit section: Roads")\]

Mexico City has a large road network, and relatively high private car usage, estimated at more than 4.5 million in 2016.^[\[179\]](#cite_note-183)^ There is an environmental program, called [Hoy No Circula](/wiki/Hoy_No_Circula "Hoy No Circula") ("Today Does Not Run", or "One Day without a Car"), whereby vehicles that have not passed emissions testing are restricted from circulating on certain days according to the ending digit of their [license plates](/wiki/Vehicle_registration_plate "Vehicle registration plate"), in an attempt to cut down on pollution and traffic congestion.^[\[180\]](#cite_note-184)^^[\[181\]](#cite_note-185)^^[\[182\]](#cite_note-186)^{#cite_ref-186}  

### Cycling {#Cycling}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&action=edit&section=36 "Edit section: Cycling")\]  
Main article: [Ecobici (Mexico City)](/wiki/Ecobici_(Mexico_City) "Ecobici (Mexico City)")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/15-07-12-Mexico-D-F-RalfR-N3S_9031.jpg/220px-15-07-12-Mexico-D-F-RalfR-N3S_9031.jpg)](/wiki/File:15-07-12-Mexico-D-F-RalfR-N3S_9031.jpg) Bicycles available for rental in [Zona Rosa](/wiki/Zona_Rosa,_Mexico_City "Zona Rosa, Mexico City")

The Mexico City local government oversees the administration of [Ecobici](/wiki/Ecobici_(Mexico_City) "Ecobici (Mexico City)"), North America's second-largest [bicycle sharing system](/wiki/Bicycle_sharing_system "Bicycle sharing system"). Established to promote sustainable urban transportation, Ecobici facilitates convenient access to bicycles for residents and visitors alike. As of September 2013, the system comprised 276 stations strategically positioned across an expansive area extending from the [Historic center](/wiki/Historic_center_of_Mexico_City "Historic center of Mexico City") to [Polanco](/wiki/Polanco_(Mexico) "Polanco (Mexico)"), a prominent district in the city. Within this network, approximately 4,000 bicycles are available for public use, enabling individuals to navigate the metropolitan landscape efficiently and reduce reliance on traditional motorized modes of transportation. Ecobici serves as a model for environmentally conscious urban mobility initiatives, reflecting Mexico City's commitment to fostering sustainable development and enhancing the quality of life for its populace.^[\[183\]](#cite_note-187)^^[\[184\]](#cite_note-188)^^[\[185\]](#cite_note-189)^{#cite_ref-189}  

Culture {#Culture}
------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&action=edit&section=37 "Edit section: Culture")\]  

### Art {#Art}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&action=edit&section=38 "Edit section: Art")\]  
Main article: [Mexican art](/wiki/Mexican_art "Mexican art")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Al_benemerito_Benito_Juarez%2C_Ciudad_de_M%C3%A9xico_MEX.jpg/220px-Al_benemerito_Benito_Juarez%2C_Ciudad_de_M%C3%A9xico_MEX.jpg)](/wiki/File:Al_benemerito_Benito_Juarez,_Ciudad_de_M%C3%A9xico_MEX.jpg) Monument "To the Meritorious [Benito Juárez](/wiki/Benito_Ju%C3%A1rez "Benito Juárez")", Mexico City

Secular works of art of this period include the [equestrian sculpture](/wiki/Equestrian_statue "Equestrian statue") of [Charles IV of Spain](/wiki/Charles_IV_of_Spain "Charles IV of Spain"), locally known as *El Caballito* ("The little horse"). This piece, in bronze, was the work of [Manuel Tolsá](/wiki/Manuel_Tols%C3%A1 "Manuel Tolsá") and it has been placed at the [Plaza Tolsá](/w/index.php?title=Plaza_Tols%C3%A1&action=edit&redlink=1 "Plaza Tolsá (page does not exist)"), in front of the [Palacio de Mineria](/wiki/Palacio_de_Mineria "Palacio de Mineria") (Mining Palace). Directly in front of this building is the [Museo Nacional de Arte](/wiki/Museo_Nacional_de_Arte "Museo Nacional de Arte") (Munal) (the National Museum of Art).
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/ReceptHallMUNAL.JPG/170px-ReceptHallMUNAL.JPG)](/wiki/File:ReceptHallMUNAL.JPG) Receptions Hall at the [Museo Nacional de Arte](/wiki/Museo_Nacional_de_Arte "Museo Nacional de Arte")

During the 19th century, an important producer of art was the [Academia de San Carlos](/wiki/Academy_of_San_Carlos "Academy of San Carlos") (San Carlos Art Academy), founded during colonial times, and which later became the Escuela Nacional de Artes Plásticas (the [National School of Arts](/wiki/National_School_of_Arts_(UNAM) "National School of Arts (UNAM)")) including painting, sculpture and graphic design, one of UNAM's [art schools](/wiki/Art_school "Art school"). Many of the works produced by the students and faculty of that time are now displayed in the Museo Nacional de San Carlos ([National Museum of San Carlos](/wiki/National_Museum_of_San_Carlos "National Museum of San Carlos")). One of the students, [José María Velasco](/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mar%C3%ADa_Velasco_G%C3%B3mez "José María Velasco Gómez"), is considered one of the greatest Mexican landscape painters of the 19th century. [Porfirio Díaz](/wiki/Porfirio_D%C3%ADaz "Porfirio Díaz")'s regime sponsored arts, especially those that followed the French school. Popular arts in the form of cartoons and illustrations flourished, e.g. those of [José Guadalupe Posada](/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Guadalupe_Posada "José Guadalupe Posada") and [Manuel Manilla](/w/index.php?title=Manuel_Manilla&action=edit&redlink=1 "Manuel Manilla (page does not exist)"). The permanent collection of the San Carlos Museum also includes paintings by European masters such as Rembrandt, Velázquez, Murillo, and Rubens.
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Monument_to_L%C3%A1zaro_C%C3%A1rdenas_%28outstretched_hand_welcoming_Spanish_immigrants%29%2C_Parque_Espa%C3%B1a%2C_col._Condesa%2C_Condesa%2C_Mexico_City.jpg/220px-Monument_to_L%C3%A1zaro_C%C3%A1rdenas_%28outstretched_hand_welcoming_Spanish_immigrants%29%2C_Parque_Espa%C3%B1a%2C_col._Condesa%2C_Condesa%2C_Mexico_City.jpg)](/wiki/File:Monument_to_L%C3%A1zaro_C%C3%A1rdenas_(outstretched_hand_welcoming_Spanish_immigrants),_Parque_Espa%C3%B1a,_col._Condesa,_Condesa,_Mexico_City.jpg) Monument to [Lázaro Cárdenas](/wiki/L%C3%A1zaro_C%C3%A1rdenas "Lázaro Cárdenas") (outstretched hand welcoming Spanish immigrants), [Parque España](/wiki/Parque_Espa%C3%B1a_(Mexico_City) "Parque España (Mexico City)")

After the [Mexican Revolution](/wiki/Mexican_Revolution "Mexican Revolution"), an [avant-garde](/wiki/Avant-garde "Avant-garde") [artistic movement](/wiki/Art_movement "Art movement") originated in Mexico City: [muralism](/wiki/Mural "Mural"). Many of the works of muralists [José Clemente Orozco](/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Clemente_Orozco "José Clemente Orozco"), [David Alfaro Siqueiros](/wiki/David_Alfaro_Siqueiros "David Alfaro Siqueiros") and [Diego Rivera](/wiki/Diego_Rivera "Diego Rivera") are displayed in numerous buildings in the city, most notably at the [National Palace](/wiki/National_Palace_(Mexico) "National Palace (Mexico)") and the [Palacio de Bellas Artes](/wiki/Palacio_de_Bellas_Artes "Palacio de Bellas Artes"). [Frida Kahlo](/wiki/Frida_Kahlo "Frida Kahlo"), wife of Rivera, with a strong nationalist expression, was also one of the most renowned of Mexican painters. Her house has become a museum that displays many of her works.^[\[186\]](#cite_note-190)^{#cite_ref-190}

The former home of Rivera muse [Dolores Olmedo](/wiki/Dolores_Olmedo "Dolores Olmedo") houses the namesake museum. The facility is in Xochimilco borough in southern Mexico City and includes several buildings surrounded by sprawling manicured lawns. It houses a large collection of Rivera and Kahlo paintings and drawings, as well as living *Xoloizcuintles* ([Mexican Hairless Dog](/wiki/Mexican_Hairless_Dog "Mexican Hairless Dog")). It also regularly hosts small but important temporary exhibits of classical and [modern art](/wiki/Modern_art "Modern art") (e.g. Venetian Masters and Contemporary New York artists).

During the 20th century, many artists immigrated to Mexico City from different regions of Mexico, such as [Leopoldo Méndez](/wiki/Leopoldo_M%C3%A9ndez "Leopoldo Méndez"), an engraver from Veracruz, who supported the creation of the socialist Taller de la Gráfica Popular ([Popular Graphics Workshop](/w/index.php?title=Popular_Graphics_Workshop&action=edit&redlink=1 "Popular Graphics Workshop (page does not exist)")), designed to help [blue-collar](/wiki/Blue-collar_worker "Blue-collar worker") workers find a venue to express their art. Other painters came from abroad, such as [Catalan](/wiki/Catalonia "Catalonia") painter [Remedios Varo](/wiki/Remedios_Varo "Remedios Varo") and other Spanish and Jewish exiles. It was in the second half of the 20th century that the artistic movement began to drift apart from the Revolutionary theme. [José Luis Cuevas](/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Luis_Cuevas "José Luis Cuevas") opted for a modernist style in contrast to the muralist movement associated with social politics.  

### Museums {#Museums}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&action=edit&section=39 "Edit section: Museums")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Hochob_Campeche_-_Rekonstruktion_des_Tempels.jpg/220px-Hochob_Campeche_-_Rekonstruktion_des_Tempels.jpg)](/wiki/File:Hochob_Campeche_-_Rekonstruktion_des_Tempels.jpg) Reconstruction of the entrance to the Hochob temple in the [National Museum of Anthropology](/wiki/National_Museum_of_Anthropology "National Museum of Anthropology") [![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Museo_Soumaya_Plaza.jpg/220px-Museo_Soumaya_Plaza.jpg)](/wiki/File:Museo_Soumaya_Plaza.jpg) [Museo Soumaya](/wiki/Museo_Soumaya "Museo Soumaya")

Mexico City has numerous museums dedicated to art, including Mexican colonial, modern and [contemporary art](/wiki/Contemporary_art "Contemporary art"), and international art. The Museo Tamayo was opened in the mid-1980s to house the collection of international contemporary art donated by Mexican painter [Rufino Tamayo](/wiki/Rufino_Tamayo "Rufino Tamayo"). The collection includes pieces by Picasso, Klee, Kandinsky, Warhol and many others, though most of the collection is stored while visiting exhibits are shown. The [Museo de Arte Moderno](/wiki/Museo_de_Arte_Moderno "Museo de Arte Moderno") is a repository of Mexican artists from the 20th century, including Rivera, Orozco, Siqueiros, Kahlo, [Gerzso](/wiki/Gunther_Gerzso "Gunther Gerzso"), Carrington, Tamayo, and also regularly hosts temporary exhibits of international modern art. In southern Mexico City, the [Carrillo Gil Museum](/w/index.php?title=Carrillo_Gil_Museum&action=edit&redlink=1 "Carrillo Gil Museum (page does not exist)") showcases avant-garde artists, as does the [Museo Universitario Arte Contemporáneo](/wiki/Museo_Universitario_Arte_Contempor%C3%A1neo "Museo Universitario Arte Contemporáneo"), designed by Mexican architect [Teodoro González de León](/wiki/Teodoro_Gonz%C3%A1lez_de_Le%C3%B3n "Teodoro González de León"), inaugurated in late 2008.

The [Museo Soumaya](/wiki/Museo_Soumaya "Museo Soumaya"), named after the wife of Mexican magnate [Carlos Slim](/wiki/Carlos_Slim "Carlos Slim"), has the largest private collection of original [Rodin](/wiki/Auguste_Rodin "Auguste Rodin") sculptures outside of France.^[\[187\]](#cite_note-191)^ It also has a large collection of [Dalí](/wiki/Dal%C3%AD "Dalí") sculptures, and recently began showing pieces in its masters collection including [El Greco](/wiki/El_Greco "El Greco"), [Velázquez](/wiki/Vel%C3%A1zquez "Velázquez"), [Picasso](/wiki/Picasso "Picasso") and [Canaletto](/wiki/Canaletto "Canaletto"). The museum inaugurated a new futuristic-design facility in 2011 just north of Polanco, while maintaining a smaller facility in [Plaza Loreto](/wiki/Museo_Soumaya#Plaza_Loreto "Museo Soumaya") in southern Mexico City. The [Colección Júmex](/wiki/Colecci%C3%B3n_J%C3%BAmex "Colección Júmex") is a contemporary art museum located on the sprawling grounds of the [Jumex](/wiki/Jumex "Jumex") juice company in the northern industrial suburb of [Ecatepec](/wiki/San_Crist%C3%B3bal_Ecatepec "San Cristóbal Ecatepec"). It has the largest private contemporary art collection in [Latin America](/wiki/Latin_America "Latin America") and hosts pieces from its permanent collection as well as traveling exhibits. The Museo de San Ildefonso, housed in the [Antiguo Colegio de San Ildefonso](/wiki/Colegio_de_San_Ildefonso,_Mexico_City "Colegio de San Ildefonso, Mexico City") in Mexico City's historic downtown district is a 17th-century colonnaded palace housing an art museum that regularly hosts world-class exhibits of Mexican and international art. The [Museo Nacional de Arte](/wiki/Museo_Nacional_de_Arte "Museo Nacional de Arte") is also located in a former palace in the historic center. It houses a large collection of pieces by all major Mexican artists of the last 400 years and also hosts visiting exhibits.{#cite_ref-191}

[Jack Kerouac](/wiki/Jack_Kerouac "Jack Kerouac"), the noted American author, spent extended periods of time in the city, and wrote his 1959 masterpiece volume of poetry *[Mexico City Blues](/wiki/Mexico_City_Blues "Mexico City Blues")* here. Another American author, [William S. Burroughs](/wiki/William_S._Burroughs "William S. Burroughs"), also lived in [Colonia Roma](/wiki/Colonia_Roma "Colonia Roma") where he accidentally shot his wife. Most of Mexico City's museums can be visited from Tuesday to Sunday from 10am to 5pm, although some of them have extended schedules, such as the Museum of Anthropology and History, which is open to 7pm. In addition to this, entrance to most museums are free on Sunday. In some cases a modest fee may be charged.^[\[188\]](#cite_note-192)^{#cite_ref-192}

The [Memory and Tolerance Museum](/wiki/Memory_and_Tolerance_Museum "Memory and Tolerance Museum"), inaugurated in 2011, showcases historical events of discrimination and genocide. Permanent exhibits include those on the Holocaust and other large-scale atrocities. It also houses temporary exhibits; one on [Tibet](/wiki/Tibet "Tibet") was inaugurated by the [Dalai Lama](/wiki/Dalai_Lama "Dalai Lama") in September 2011.^[\[189\]](#cite_note-193)^{#cite_ref-193}  

### Music, theater and entertainment {#Music.2C_theater_and_entertainment}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&action=edit&section=40 "Edit section: Music, theater and entertainment")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Teatro_de_la_Ciudad_de_Mexico.jpg/170px-Teatro_de_la_Ciudad_de_Mexico.jpg)](/wiki/File:Teatro_de_la_Ciudad_de_Mexico.jpg) The [City Theatre](/wiki/Teatro_de_la_Ciudad "Teatro de la Ciudad") built in 1918  
Further information: [Music of Mexico](/wiki/Music_of_Mexico "Music of Mexico") and [Regional styles of Mexican music](/wiki/Regional_styles_of_Mexican_music "Regional styles of Mexican music")

Mexico City is home to a number of orchestras offering season programs. These include the [Mexico City Philharmonic](/wiki/Mexico_City_Philharmonic "Mexico City Philharmonic"),^[\[190\]](#cite_note-194)^ which performs at the Sala Ollin Yoliztli; the [National Symphony Orchestra](/wiki/National_Symphony_Orchestra_(Mexico) "National Symphony Orchestra (Mexico)"), whose home base is the [Palacio de Bellas Artes](/wiki/Palacio_de_Bellas_Artes "Palacio de Bellas Artes") (Palace of the [Fine Arts](/wiki/Fine_art "Fine art")), a masterpiece of [art nouveau](/wiki/Art_Nouveau "Art Nouveau") and art decó styles; the [Philharmonic Orchestra](/wiki/Orchestra "Orchestra") of the National Autonomous University of Mexico ([OFUNAM](/wiki/OFUNAM "OFUNAM")),^[\[191\]](#cite_note-195)^ and the [Minería Symphony Orchestra](/w/index.php?title=Miner%C3%ADa_Symphony_Orchestra&action=edit&redlink=1 "Minería Symphony Orchestra (page does not exist)"),^[\[192\]](#cite_note-196)^ both of which perform at the [Sala Nezahualcóyotl](/w/index.php?title=Sala_Nezahualc%C3%B3yotl&action=edit&redlink=1 "Sala Nezahualcóyotl (page does not exist)"), which was the first wrap-around concert hall in the Western Hemisphere when inaugurated in 1976. There are also many smaller ensembles that enrich the city's musical scene, including the [Carlos Chávez Youth Symphony](/w/index.php?title=Carlos_Ch%C3%A1vez_Youth_Symphony&action=edit&redlink=1 "Carlos Chávez Youth Symphony (page does not exist)"), the [Cuarteto Latinoamericano](/wiki/Cuarteto_Latinoamericano "Cuarteto Latinoamericano"), the [New World Orchestra](/wiki/New_World_Symphony_Orchestra "New World Symphony Orchestra") (Orquesta del Nuevo Mundo), the [National Polytechnical Symphony](/w/index.php?title=National_Polytechnical_Symphony&action=edit&redlink=1 "National Polytechnical Symphony (page does not exist)") and the [Bellas Artes Chamber Orchestra](/w/index.php?title=Bellas_Artes_Chamber_Orchestra&action=edit&redlink=1 "Bellas Artes Chamber Orchestra (page does not exist)") (Orquesta de Cámara de Bellas Artes).{#cite_ref-196}

The city is also a leading center of [popular culture](/wiki/Popular_culture "Popular culture") and music. There are a multitude of venues hosting Spanish and foreign-language performers. These include the 10,000-seat [National Auditorium](/wiki/National_Auditorium "National Auditorium") that regularly schedules the Spanish and English-language pop and rock artists, as well as many of the world's leading [performing arts](/wiki/Performing_arts "Performing arts") ensembles, the auditorium also broadcasts [grand opera](/wiki/Grand_opera "Grand opera") performances from New York's [Metropolitan Opera](/wiki/Metropolitan_Opera "Metropolitan Opera") on giant, high definition screens. In 2007 National Auditorium was selected world's best venue by multiple genre media. Other sites for pop-artist performances include the 3,000-seat [Teatro Metropolitan](/wiki/Teatro_Metropolitan "Teatro Metropolitan"), the 15,000-seat [Palacio de los Deportes](/wiki/Palacio_de_los_Deportes "Palacio de los Deportes"), and the larger 50,000-seat [Foro Sol](/wiki/Foro_Sol "Foro Sol") Stadium, where popular international artists perform on a regular basis. The [Cirque du Soleil](/wiki/Cirque_du_Soleil "Cirque du Soleil") has held several seasons at the [Carpa Santa Fe](/w/index.php?title=Carpa_Santa_Fe&action=edit&redlink=1 "Carpa Santa Fe (page does not exist)"), in the [Santa Fe](/wiki/Santa_Fe_(Mexico_City) "Santa Fe (Mexico City)") district in the western part of the city. There are numerous venues for smaller musical ensembles and solo performers. These include the [Hard Rock Live](/wiki/Hard_Rock_Live "Hard Rock Live"), Bataclán, Foro Scotiabank, Lunario, Circo Volador and Voilá Acoustique. Recent additions include the 20,000-seat [Arena Ciudad de México](/wiki/Arena_Ciudad_de_M%C3%A9xico "Arena Ciudad de México"), the 3,000-seat Pepsi Center World Trade Center, and the 2,500-seat Auditorio Blackberry.

The Centro Nacional de las Artes ([National Center for the Arts](/w/index.php?title=National_Center_for_the_Arts&action=edit&redlink=1 "National Center for the Arts (page does not exist)")) has several venues for music, theater, dance. UNAM's main campus, also in the southern part of the city, is home to the Centro Cultural Universitario (the [University Culture Center](/w/index.php?title=University_Culture_Center&action=edit&redlink=1 "University Culture Center (page does not exist)")) (CCU). The CCU also houses the [National Library](/wiki/National_Library_of_Mexico "National Library of Mexico"), the interactive [Universum, Museo de las Ciencias](/wiki/Universum_(UNAM) "Universum (UNAM)"),^[\[193\]](#cite_note-197)^ the Sala Nezahualcóyotl concert hall, several theaters and cinemas, and the new [University Museum of Contemporary Art](/wiki/Museo_Universitario_Arte_Contempor%C3%A1neo "Museo Universitario Arte Contemporáneo") (MUAC).^[\[194\]](#cite_note-198)^ A branch of the National University's CCU cultural center was inaugurated in 2007 in the facilities of the former [Ministry of Foreign Affairs](/wiki/Foreign_minister "Foreign minister"), known as Tlatelolco, in north-central Mexico City.{#cite_ref-198}
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Biblioteca_Vasconcelos%2C_Ciudad_de_M%C3%A9xico%2C_M%C3%A9xico%2C_2015-07-20%2C_DD_16-18_HDR.JPG/220px-Biblioteca_Vasconcelos%2C_Ciudad_de_M%C3%A9xico%2C_M%C3%A9xico%2C_2015-07-20%2C_DD_16-18_HDR.JPG)](/wiki/File:Biblioteca_Vasconcelos,_Ciudad_de_M%C3%A9xico,_M%C3%A9xico,_2015-07-20,_DD_16-18_HDR.JPG) [Biblioteca Vasconcelos](/wiki/Biblioteca_Vasconcelos "Biblioteca Vasconcelos")

The [José Vasconcelos Library](/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Vasconcelos_Library "José Vasconcelos Library"), a national library, is located on the grounds of the former Buenavista railroad station in the northern part of the city. The [Papalote Museo del Niño](/wiki/Papalote_Museo_del_Ni%C3%B1o "Papalote Museo del Niño") (Kite Children's Museum), which houses the world's largest dome screen, is located in the wooded park of [Chapultepec](/wiki/Chapultepec "Chapultepec"), near the [Museo Tecnológico](/w/index.php?title=Museo_Tecnol%C3%B3gico&action=edit&redlink=1 "Museo Tecnológico (page does not exist)"), and [Aztlán Parque Urbano](/wiki/Aztl%C3%A1n_Parque_Urbano "Aztlán Parque Urbano"), an [amusement park](/wiki/Amusement_park "Amusement park"). The theme park [Six Flags México](/wiki/Six_Flags_M%C3%A9xico "Six Flags México") (the largest amusement park in Latin America) is located in the [Ajusco](/wiki/Ajusco "Ajusco") neighborhood, in Tlalpan borough, southern Mexico City. During the winter, the main square of the [Zócalo](/wiki/Z%C3%B3calo "Zócalo") is transformed into a gigantic [ice skating rink](/wiki/Ice_rink "Ice rink"), which is said to be the largest in the world behind that of Moscow's [Red Square](/wiki/Red_Square "Red Square").

The [Cineteca Nacional](/w/index.php?title=Cineteca_Nacional&action=edit&redlink=1 "Cineteca Nacional (page does not exist)") \[[es](https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cineteca_Nacional "es:Cineteca Nacional")\] (Mexican Film Library), near the Coyoacán suburb, shows a variety of films, and stages many film festivals, including the annual [International Showcase](/w/index.php?title=Muestra_Internacional_de_Cine&action=edit&redlink=1 "Muestra Internacional de Cine (page does not exist)"), and many smaller ones ranging from Scandinavian and Uruguayan cinema, to Jewish and LGBT-themed films. [Cinépolis](/wiki/Cin%C3%A9polis "Cinépolis") and [Cinemex](/wiki/Cinemex "Cinemex"), the two biggest film [business chains](/wiki/Chain_store "Chain store"), also have several film festivals throughout the year, with both national and international movies. Mexico City has a number of [IMAX](/wiki/IMAX "IMAX") theaters, providing residents and visitors access to films ranging from documentaries to blockbusters on these large screens.  

### Cuisine {#Cuisine}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&action=edit&section=41 "Edit section: Cuisine")\]  
Main article: [Cuisine of Mexico City](/wiki/Cuisine_of_Mexico_City "Cuisine of Mexico City")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Street_tacos_in_Mexico_City_%288495923877%29.jpg/220px-Street_tacos_in_Mexico_City_%288495923877%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Street_tacos_in_Mexico_City_(8495923877).jpg) [Street](/wiki/Street_food "Street food") [tacos](/wiki/Tacos "Tacos") in Mexico City

Once considered plebeian fare, by the 19th century [tacos](/wiki/Taco "Taco") had become a standard of Mexico City's cuisine. Furthermore, as authorities struggled to tax local [taquerias](/wiki/Taquerias "Taquerias"), imposing licensing requirements and penalties, they recorded some details of the types of foods being served by these establishments. The most frequent reference was for *tacos de [barbacoa](/wiki/Barbacoa "Barbacoa")* . Also mentioned are [enchiladas](/wiki/Enchiladas "Enchiladas"), *tacos de minero* and *[gorditas](/wiki/Gorditas "Gorditas")* , along with oyster shops and fried fish stands. There is evidence of some regional specialties being made available for recent migrants; at least two shops were known to serve *[pozole](/wiki/Pozole "Pozole")* , a type of stew similar to [hominy](/wiki/Hominy "Hominy") that is a staple of [Guadalajara](/wiki/Guadalajara "Guadalajara"), [Jalisco](/wiki/Jalisco "Jalisco").^[\[195\]](#cite_note-199)^ Mexico City is known for having some of the freshest fish and seafood in Mexico's interior. [La Nueva Viga Market](/wiki/La_Nueva_Viga_Market "La Nueva Viga Market") is the second largest seafood market in the world after the [Tsukiji fish market](/wiki/Tsukiji_fish_market "Tsukiji fish market") in Japan.{#cite_ref-199}

Mexico City offers a variety of cuisines: restaurants specializing in the regional cuisines of Mexico's 31 states are available in the city, and the city also has several branches of internationally recognized restaurants. These include Paris' Au Pied de Cochon and [Brasserie Lipp](/wiki/Brasserie_Lipp "Brasserie Lipp"), Philippe (by Philippe Chow); Nobu, [Quintonil](/wiki/Quintonil "Quintonil"), Morimoto; Pámpano, owned by Mexican-raised opera singer [Plácido Domingo](/wiki/Pl%C3%A1cido_Domingo "Plácido Domingo"). There are branches of [Japanese](/wiki/Japanese_cuisine "Japanese cuisine") restaurant [Suntory](/wiki/Suntory "Suntory"), [Italian](/wiki/Italian_cuisine "Italian cuisine") restaurant Alfredo, as well as New York steakhouses [Morton's](/wiki/Morton%27s_Restaurant_Group "Morton's Restaurant Group") and [The Palm](/wiki/The_Palm_(restaurant) "The Palm (restaurant)"), and Monte Carlo's BeefBar. Three of [Lima](/wiki/Lima,_Peru "Lima, Peru")'s [Haute](/wiki/Haute_cuisine "Haute cuisine") restaurants, serving [Peruvian cuisine](/wiki/Peruvian_cuisine "Peruvian cuisine"), have locations in Mexico City: La Mar, Segundo Muelle and Astrid y Gastón.

For the 2023 list of [World's 50 Best Restaurants](/wiki/Restaurant_(magazine)_Top_50 "Restaurant (magazine) Top 50") as named by the British magazine *[Restaurant](/wiki/Restaurant_(magazine) "Restaurant (magazine)")* , Mexico City ranked 13th best with the Mexican avant-garde restaurant [Pujol](/wiki/Pujol_(restaurant) "Pujol (restaurant)") (owned by Mexican chef [Enrique Olvera](/wiki/Enrique_Olvera "Enrique Olvera")).^[\[196\]](#cite_note-200)^ Also notable is the Basque-Mexican fusion restaurant [Biko](/wiki/Biko_(restaurant) "Biko (restaurant)") (run and co-owned by Bruno Oteiza and Mikel Alonso), which placed outside the list at 59th, but in previous years has ranked within the top 50.^[\[197\]](#cite_note-2014_awards-201)^ Other that has been placed on the list in 2019 is the restaurant Sud 777 at 58th place.^[\[198\]](#cite_note-202)^ In 2024, [seven restaurants in the city](/wiki/List_of_Michelin-starred_restaurants_in_Mexico "List of Michelin-starred restaurants in Mexico") received [Michelin stars](/wiki/Michelin_stars "Michelin stars").^[\[199\]](#cite_note-203)^ At the other end of the scale are working class [pulque](/wiki/Pulque "Pulque") bars known as *pulquerías*, a challenge for tourists to locate and experience.{#cite_ref-203}  

### Media {#Media}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&action=edit&section=42 "Edit section: Media")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/TELEVISA_CHAPULTEPEC.jpg/220px-TELEVISA_CHAPULTEPEC.jpg)](/wiki/File:TELEVISA_CHAPULTEPEC.jpg) [Televisa](/wiki/Televisa "Televisa") headquarters in Mexico City

Mexico City is Mexico's most important hub for the printed media and [book publishing](/wiki/Publishing "Publishing") industries. Dozens of daily newspapers are published, including *[El Universal](/wiki/El_Universal_(Mexico_City) "El Universal (Mexico City)")* , *[Excélsior](/wiki/Exc%C3%A9lsior "Excélsior")* , *[Reforma](/wiki/Reforma_(newspaper) "Reforma (newspaper)")* and *[La Jornada](/wiki/La_Jornada "La Jornada")* . Other major papers include *[Milenio](/wiki/Milenio "Milenio")* , *[Crónica](/wiki/Cr%C3%B3nica,_Buenos_Aires "Crónica, Buenos Aires")* , *[El Economista](/wiki/El_Economista_(Mexico) "El Economista (Mexico)")* and *[El Financiero](/wiki/El_Financiero "El Financiero")*.^[\[200\]](#cite_note-SIC-204)^^[\[201\]](#cite_note-205)^ Leading magazines include *[Expansión](/wiki/Expansi%C3%B3n_(Mexico) "Expansión (Mexico)")*, *[Proceso](/wiki/Proceso_(magazine) "Proceso (magazine)")* , *[Poder](/w/index.php?title=Poder&action=edit&redlink=1 "Poder (page does not exist)")* , as well as dozens of entertainment publications such as *[Vanidades](/wiki/Vanidades "Vanidades")* , *[Quién](/w/index.php?title=Qui%C3%A9n_(magazine)&action=edit&redlink=1 "Quién (magazine) (page does not exist)")* , *[Chilango](/wiki/Chilango "Chilango")* , *[TV Notas](/w/index.php?title=TVNotas&action=edit&redlink=1 "TVNotas (page does not exist)")* , and local editions of *[Vogue](/wiki/Vogue_(magazine) "Vogue (magazine)")* , *[GQ](/wiki/GQ "GQ")* , and *[Architectural Digest](/wiki/Architectural_Digest "Architectural Digest")*.{#cite_ref-205}

It is also a leading center of the [advertising industry](/wiki/Advertising_industry "Advertising industry"). Most international ad firms have offices in the city, including Grey, [JWT](/wiki/J._Walter_Thompson "J. Walter Thompson"), [Leo Burnett](/wiki/Leo_Burnett "Leo Burnett"), [Euro RSCG](/wiki/Euro_RSCG "Euro RSCG"), [BBDO](/wiki/BBDO "BBDO"), Ogilvy, [Saatchi \& Saatchi](/wiki/Saatchi_%26_Saatchi "Saatchi & Saatchi"), and [McCann Erickson](/wiki/McCann_Erickson "McCann Erickson"). Many local firms also compete in the sector, including [Alazraki](/wiki/Alazraki "Alazraki"), [Olabuenaga/Chemistri](/w/index.php?title=Olabuenaga/Chemistri&action=edit&redlink=1 "Olabuenaga/Chemistri (page does not exist)"), Terán, Augusto Elías, and Clemente Cámara, among others. There are 60 [radio stations](/wiki/List_of_radio_stations_in_Mexico_City "List of radio stations in Mexico City") operating in the city and many [local community](/wiki/Local_community "Local community") radio transmission networks.

The two largest media companies in the Spanish-speaking world, [Televisa](/wiki/Televisa "Televisa") and [TV Azteca](/wiki/TV_Azteca "TV Azteca"), are headquartered in Mexico City. [Televisa](/wiki/Televisa "Televisa") often presents itself as the largest producer of Spanish-language content.^[\[202\]](#cite_note-206)^ Other [local television](/wiki/Television_channel "Television channel") channels include:{#cite_ref-206}

[XHDF](/wiki/XHDF-TDT "XHDF-TDT") 1 (Azteca Uno),^[\[203\]](#cite_note-207)^ [XEW](/wiki/XEW-TDT "XEW-TDT") 2 (Televisa W),^[\[204\]](#cite_note-208)^ [XHCTMX](/wiki/XHCTMX-TDT "XHCTMX-TDT") 3, [XHTV](/wiki/XHTV-TDT "XHTV-TDT") 4, [XHGC](/wiki/XHGC-TDT "XHGC-TDT") 5, [XHTDMX](/wiki/XHTDMX-TDT "XHTDMX-TDT") 6, [XHIMT](/wiki/XHIMT-TDT "XHIMT-TDT") 7, [XEQ](/wiki/XEQ-TDT "XEQ-TDT") 9, [XEIPN](/wiki/XEIPN-TDT "XEIPN-TDT") 11, [XHUNAM](/wiki/XHUNAM-TDT "XHUNAM-TDT") 20, [XHCDM](/wiki/XHCDM-TDT "XHCDM-TDT") 21, [XEIMT](/wiki/XEIMT-TDT "XEIMT-TDT") 22, [XHTRES](/wiki/XHTRES-TDT "XHTRES-TDT") 28, [XHTVM](/wiki/XHTVM-TDT "XHTVM-TDT") 40 and [XHHCU](/wiki/XHHCU-TDT "XHHCU-TDT") 45.{#cite_ref-208}  

### Sports {#Sports}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&action=edit&section=43 "Edit section: Sports")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/ACMX16.JPG/220px-ACMX16.JPG)](/wiki/File:ACMX16.JPG) [Mexico City Arena](/wiki/Mexico_City_Arena "Mexico City Arena")

[Association football](/wiki/Association_football "Association football") is the country's most popular and most [televised franchised sport](/wiki/Broadcasting_of_sports_events "Broadcasting of sports events"). Its important venues in Mexico City include the [Azteca Stadium](/wiki/Azteca_Stadium "Azteca Stadium"), home to the [Mexico national football team](/wiki/Mexico_national_football_team "Mexico national football team") and giants [América](/wiki/Club_Am%C3%A9rica "Club América") and [Cruz Azul](/wiki/Cruz_Azul "Cruz Azul"), which can seat 91,653 fans, making it the biggest stadium in Latin America. The [Olympic Stadium](/wiki/Estadio_Ol%C3%ADmpico_Universitario "Estadio Olímpico Universitario") in [Ciudad Universitaria](/wiki/University_City_of_Mexico "University City of Mexico") is home to the football club giants [Universidad Nacional](/wiki/Club_Universidad_Nacional "Club Universidad Nacional"), with a [seating capacity](/wiki/Seating_capacity "Seating capacity") of over 52,000. The [Sports City Stadium](/wiki/Estadio_Ciudad_de_los_Deportes "Estadio Ciudad de los Deportes"), which seats 33,042 fans, is near the [World Trade Center Mexico City](/wiki/World_Trade_Center_Mexico_City "World Trade Center Mexico City") in the Nochebuena [neighborhood](/wiki/Colonia_(Mexico) "Colonia (Mexico)"), and is home to the historical [Atlante](/wiki/Atlante_F.C. "Atlante F.C."). América, Cruz Azul and Universidad Nacional are based in Mexico City and play in the [First Division](/wiki/Primera_Divisi%C3%B3n_de_M%C3%A9xico "Primera División de México"); they are also part, with Guadalajara-based giants [Club Deportivo Guadalajara](/wiki/Club_Deportivo_Guadalajara "Club Deportivo Guadalajara"), of Mexico's traditional "Big Four". The city's three derbies are the "[Clásico Joven](/wiki/Cl%C3%A1sico_Joven "Clásico Joven")", played between América and Cruz Azul, the capital's two most popular and successful teams; the "[Clásico Capitalino](/wiki/Cl%C3%A1sico_Capitalino "Clásico Capitalino")", between América and Universidad Nacional, and the "Clásico Metropolitano", between Cruz Azul and Universidad Nacional.

The country hosted the [FIFA World Cup](/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup "FIFA World Cup") in [1970](/wiki/1970_FIFA_World_Cup "1970 FIFA World Cup") and [1986](/wiki/1986_FIFA_World_Cup "1986 FIFA World Cup"), and Azteca Stadium is the first stadium in World Cup history to host the final twice. The city will be one of the host cities for the [2026 FIFA World Cup](/wiki/2026_FIFA_World_Cup "2026 FIFA World Cup").^[\[205\]](#cite_note-209)^ Mexico City is the first Latin American city to host the Olympic Games, having held the [Summer Olympics](/wiki/1968_Summer_Olympics "1968 Summer Olympics") in 1968, winning bids against [Buenos Aires](/wiki/Buenos_Aires "Buenos Aires"), [Lyon](/wiki/Lyon "Lyon") and Detroit. The city hosted the 1955 and 1975 [Pan American Games](/wiki/Pan_American_Games "Pan American Games"), the latter after Santiago and São Paulo withdrew. The [ICF Flatwater Racing World Championships](/wiki/ICF_Canoe_Sprint_World_Championships "ICF Canoe Sprint World Championships") were hosted here in 1974 and 1994. [Lucha libre](/wiki/Lucha_libre "Lucha libre") is a Mexican style of wrestling, and is one of the more popular sports throughout the country. The main venues in the city are [Arena México](/wiki/Arena_M%C3%A9xico "Arena México") and [Arena Coliseo](/wiki/Arena_Coliseo "Arena Coliseo").{#cite_ref-209}
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Aut%C3%B3dromo_Hermanos_Rodr%C3%ADguez%2C_June_4%2C_2018_SkySat_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Aut%C3%B3dromo_Hermanos_Rodr%C3%ADguez%2C_June_4%2C_2018_SkySat_%28cropped%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Aut%C3%B3dromo_Hermanos_Rodr%C3%ADguez,_June_4,_2018_SkySat_(cropped).jpg) [Autódromo Hermanos Rodríguez](/wiki/Aut%C3%B3dromo_Hermanos_Rodr%C3%ADguez "Autódromo Hermanos Rodríguez")

The [Autódromo Hermanos Rodríguez](/wiki/Aut%C3%B3dromo_Hermanos_Rodr%C3%ADguez "Autódromo Hermanos Rodríguez") is the main venue for motorsport, and hosts the Formula 1 [Mexican Grand Prix](/wiki/Mexican_Grand_Prix "Mexican Grand Prix") since its return to the sport in 2015, the event being held in the past from 1962 to 1970, and again from 1986 to 1992. Since 2016, it also hosts the Formula E [Mexico City ePrix](/wiki/Mexico_City_ePrix "Mexico City ePrix"). From 1980 to 1981 and again from 2002 to 2007, the circuit hosted the [Champ Car](/wiki/Champ_Car "Champ Car") World Series [Gran Premio de México](/wiki/Gran_Premio_de_M%C3%A9xico "Gran Premio de México"). Beginning in 2005, the [NASCAR](/wiki/NASCAR "NASCAR") [Nationwide Series](/wiki/Nationwide_Series "Nationwide Series") ran the [Telcel-Motorola México 200](/wiki/Corona_M%C3%A9xico_200 "Corona México 200"). 2005 also marked the first running of the Mexico City 250 by the [Grand-Am](/wiki/Grand_American_Road_Racing_Association "Grand American Road Racing Association") [Rolex Sports Car Series](/wiki/Rolex_Sports_Car_Series "Rolex Sports Car Series"). Both races were removed from their series' schedules for 2009.

Baseball is another sport played professionally in the city. Mexico City is home of the [Mexico City Red Devils](/wiki/Diablos_Rojos_del_M%C3%A9xico "Diablos Rojos del México") of the [Mexican League](/wiki/Mexican_League_(baseball) "Mexican League (baseball)"), which is considered a Triple-A league by Major League Baseball. The Devils play their home games at [Estadio Alfredo Harp Helú](/wiki/Estadio_Alfredo_Harp_Hel%C3%BA "Estadio Alfredo Harp Helú")^[\[206\]](#cite_note-210)^ designed by international Mexican-American architect [FGP Atelier](http://fgp-atelier.com) Founder [Francisco Gonzalez Pulido](/wiki/Francisco_Gonzalez_Pulido "Francisco Gonzalez Pulido") in collaboration with local architect Taller ADG. Mexico City has some 10 Little Leagues for young baseball players. In 2005, Mexico City became the first city to host an [NFL](/wiki/National_Football_League "National Football League") regular season game outside of the United States, at the [Azteca Stadium](/wiki/Estadio_Azteca "Estadio Azteca"). The crowd of 103,467 people attending this game was the largest ever for a regular season game in NFL history until 2009.^[\[207\]](#cite_note-211)^{#cite_ref-211}

The city has also hosted several [NBA](/wiki/National_Basketball_Association "National Basketball Association") pre-season games and has hosted international basketball's [FIBA Americas Championship](/wiki/FIBA_Americas_Championship "FIBA Americas Championship"), along with north-of-the-border Major League Baseball exhibition games at [Foro Sol](/wiki/Foro_Sol "Foro Sol"). In 2017, NBA commissioner [Adam Silver](/wiki/Adam_Silver "Adam Silver") expressed interest in placing an [NBA G League](/wiki/NBA_G_League "NBA G League") expansion team in Mexico City as early as 2018. This came to fruition on 12 December 2019 when commissioner Silver announced at a press conference in [Mexico City Arena](/wiki/Mexico_City_Arena "Mexico City Arena") that [LNBP](/wiki/Liga_Nacional_de_Baloncesto_Profesional "Liga Nacional de Baloncesto Profesional") team, [Capitanes de Ciudad de México](/wiki/Capitanes_de_Ciudad_de_M%C3%A9xico "Capitanes de Ciudad de México") will be joining the G League in the 2020--21 season on a five-year agreement.

Other sports facilities in Mexico City are the [Palacio de los Deportes](/wiki/Palacio_de_los_Deportes "Palacio de los Deportes") indoor arena, [Francisco Márquez Olympic Swimming Pool](/wiki/Alberca_Ol%C3%ADmpica_Francisco_M%C3%A1rquez "Alberca Olímpica Francisco Márquez"), the [Hipódromo de Las Américas](/wiki/Hip%C3%B3dromo_de_las_Am%C3%A9ricas "Hipódromo de las Américas"), the [Agustin Melgar Olympic Velodrome](/wiki/Agustin_Melgar_Olympic_Velodrome "Agustin Melgar Olympic Velodrome"), and venues for equestrianism and horse racing, ice hockey, [rugby](/wiki/Rugby_football "Rugby football"), American-style football, baseball, and basketball. [Bullfighting](/wiki/Bullfighting "Bullfighting") takes place every Sunday during bullfighting season at the 50,000-seat [Plaza México](/wiki/Plaza_M%C3%A9xico "Plaza México"), the world's largest bullring. Mexico City's [golf courses](/wiki/Golf_course "Golf course") have hosted Women's [LPGA](/wiki/LPGA "LPGA") action, and two [Men's Golf World Cups](/wiki/World_Cup_(men%27s_golf) "World Cup (men's golf)"). Courses throughout the city are available as private as well as public venues.  
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Sobrevuelos_CDMX_IMG_5971_%2825513748117%29.jpg/200px-Sobrevuelos_CDMX_IMG_5971_%2825513748117%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Sobrevuelos_CDMX_IMG_5971_(25513748117).jpg)  
[Azteca Stadium](/wiki/Azteca_Stadium "Azteca Stadium")  
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Estadio_Ol%C3%ADmpico_Universitario_-_5.jpg/220px-Estadio_Ol%C3%ADmpico_Universitario_-_5.jpg)](/wiki/File:Estadio_Ol%C3%ADmpico_Universitario_-_5.jpg)  
[University Olympic Stadium](/wiki/Estadio_Ol%C3%ADmpico_Universitario "Estadio Olímpico Universitario")  
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Sobrevuelos_CDMX_HJ2A6001_%2840386252041%29.jpg/200px-Sobrevuelos_CDMX_HJ2A6001_%2840386252041%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Sobrevuelos_CDMX_HJ2A6001_(40386252041).jpg)  
[Sports City Stadium](/wiki/Estadio_Ciudad_de_los_Deportes "Estadio Ciudad de los Deportes")  
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Estadio_Alfredo_Harp_Hel%C3%BA_%282019%29.jpg/180px-Estadio_Alfredo_Harp_Hel%C3%BA_%282019%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Estadio_Alfredo_Harp_Hel%C3%BA_(2019).jpg)  
[Alfredo Harp Helú Stadium](/wiki/Estadio_Alfredo_Harp_Hel%C3%BA "Estadio Alfredo Harp Helú")

Notes {#Notes}
--------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Mexico_City&action=edit&section=45 "Edit section: Notes")\]  
1. **[\^](#cite_ref-a_1-0)**
   * *Mexihco Hueyaltepetl* ([Central Nahuatl](/wiki/Central_Nahuatl_language "Central Nahuatl language"))
   * *U noj kaajil México* ([Yucatec Maya](/wiki/Yucatec_Maya_language "Yucatec Maya language"))
   * *'Monda* ([Otomian languages](/wiki/Otomian_languages "Otomian languages"))
   {#cite_note-a-1}
{#cite_note-a-1}
2. **[\^](#cite_ref-12)** In [Peninsular Spanish](/wiki/Peninsular_Spanish "Peninsular Spanish"), the spelling variant *Méjico* , is also used alongside *México* . According to the *[Diccionario panhispánico de dudas](/wiki/Diccionario_panhisp%C3%A1nico_de_dudas "Diccionario panhispánico de dudas")* by the [Royal Spanish Academy](/wiki/Royal_Spanish_Academy "Royal Spanish Academy") and the [Association of Academies of the Spanish Language](/wiki/Association_of_Academies_of_the_Spanish_Language "Association of Academies of the Spanish Language"), the spelling version with J is correct, however, the spelling with X is recommended, as it is the one is used in [Mexican Spanish](/wiki/Mexican_Spanish "Mexican Spanish").{#cite_note-12}
{#cite_note-12}
3. **[\^](#cite_ref-16)** Spanish: *Ciudad de México* ,  'City of Mexico',^[\[b\]](#cite_note-12)^^[\[11\]](#cite_note-13)^ locally [\[sjuˈða(ð) ðe ˈmexiko\]](/wiki/Help:IPA/Spanish "Help:IPA/Spanish") [](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/2/26/Es-Ciudad_de_Mexico.ogg/Es-Ciudad_de_Mexico.ogg.mp3 "Play audio")^[ⓘ](/wiki/File:Es-Ciudad_de_Mexico.ogg "File:Es-Ciudad de Mexico.ogg")^; abbr.: **CDMX** ; [Central Nahuatl](/wiki/Central_Nahuatl_languages "Central Nahuatl languages"): *Mexihco Hueyaltepetl*,^[\[12\]](#cite_note-14)^ Nahuatl pronunciation: [\[meːˈʃiʔko wejaːlˈtepeːt͡ɬ\]](/wiki/Help:IPA/Nahuatl "Help:IPA/Nahuatl");^[\[13\]](#cite_note-15)^ [Otomi](/wiki/Otomi_language "Otomi language"): *'Monda*{#cite_ref-15}
{#cite_note-16}
4. **[\^](#cite_ref-25)** [Quito](/wiki/Quito "Quito"), the capital city of [Ecuador](/wiki/Ecuador "Ecuador"), is the other such city.{#cite_note-25}
{#cite_note-25}
